### Post by dean10 on 2014-05-11
New to the forum, so hi to all.
Also, new to Ubuntu and liking it at first glance.
A mac user for many years, with a disdain for windows.
Putting Ubuntu on my son's HP Pavilion G6 after a recommendation from a friend who works in IT.

The HP unit came pre installed with W7 and had all four permissible partitions allocated to...
C drive; HP-tools; Recovery; System.

After much thread reading, I followed the advice and method here... 
[http://h30434.www3.hp.com/t5/Other-Notebook-PC-Questions/How-to-repartition-HDD-of-HP-notebook-with-pre-installed/td-p/742019](http://h30434.www3.hp.com/t5/Other-Notebook-PC-Questions/How-to-repartition-HDD-of-HP-notebook-with-pre-installed/td-p/742019)

It was a long and laborious process, with many linked sub threads to follow, which I carried out - to the letter.
I ended up with a successful install of Ubuntu, however this is not on the partition I thought it would be!

Initially, the Ubuntu disc would not install the OS.
I discovered this was due to the max number of partitions (above) being allocated.
I deleted the "system" partition according to: [http://www.sevenforums.com/tutorials/71363-system-reserved-partition-delete.html?ltr=S](http://www.sevenforums.com/tutorials/71363-system-reserved-partition-delete.html?ltr=S)
Then used PartitionWizard to shrink "C" and create a new partition on which to install Ubuntu.
But I think I got lost somewhere because I still had the 200mb partition left over from "System" plus an additional partition of about 200gb which
I got from shrinking "C" and creating, and then the Ubuntu installer ignored these and went on to split "C" further!

Here's a diskmgmt screenshot...

[ATTACH=CONFIG]253065[/ATTACH]

My question is how can I get Ubuntu into the large "ntfs1 (G)" volume I created and lose the 2 unlabelled volumes left over from removing the system volume and the one that the Ubuntu installer created?

Many thanks.
Please ask if further info required.

---

### Post by coffeecat on 2014-05-11
Welcome to the forum. 

A few things:

You can't install Ubuntu to a NTFS partition &#8211; Linux has its own filesystems and although it can read and write to NTFS, you cannot use NTFS for a running system. Except &#8211; well &#8211; wubi is a way of installing Ubuntu to a pseudo-partition within an NTFS filesystem, but I really hope you didn't install with wubi. Did you install from a live DVD or USB session, or did you download a Windows installer?

I rather think that you did not use wubi and that you have a couple of Linux logical partitions, except that the Windows Disk Management gets horribly confused with Linux logical partitions and calls them healthy primary partitions without having a guess as to what the filesystem might be. I see 232.25 GB and 5.47GB partitions thus described in your screenshot and I suspect they may be your Ubuntu root and swap partitions. But let's confirm...

Can you boot into Ubuntu? If so, do so, install Gparted from the Software Centre (you need to be connected to the internet) and take a screenshot (alt+PrtScrn) of the Gparted window and post that.

If you can't connect to the internet, open a terminal and post the output of these two terminal commands:

```
sudo fdisk -l
mount
```

You can open a terminal with ctrl-alt-t and when prompted for your password with the first command, just type it in and then press enter. Your password won't be echoed to the screen but it is going in.

---

### Post by Impavidus on 2014-05-11
The Windows partition editor is a bit clumsy. It's good at resizing Windows partitions but that's about it. The partitions as shown by the Windows partition editor are simply impossible. A screenshot from gparted would be useful. Instead of booting your installed Ubuntu and installing gparted, you can also boot your live disk and start gparted from there. It should be available by default on a live disk (at least, it's on my latest Xubuntu live disk) and if you want to change the partitions you'll need a live disk anyway.

---

### Post by coffeecat on 2014-05-11
Personally, I wouldn't do anything about your setup - at least for the time being. Apart from having an overlarge Ubuntu root partition, you have a nice partition layout. Compare the Gparted output with your Windows Disk Management to see what both Impavidus and I were saying about Disk Management not being up to task when in the presence of Linux partitions. In detail:

sda1 - Your Windows C: partition. 

sda5 - your Ubuntu root partition. At 232 GB it's far too large unless you keep a lot of stuff in your home folder. But you have a partition that you could use as a shared Data partition (see below). Ubuntu is much less profligate that Windows in HD usage. You'd be hard put to go much above even 15GB with just the operating system and installed applications, unless you install games.

sda6 - your Ubuntu swap partition. Linux uses a swap partition (as a default) rather than the swap file that Windows does.

sda7 - a NTFS logical partition, designated G:/ by Windows. Seeing as you originally intended it for Ubuntu, and it's not needed for that, what a happy accident. If that was my machine I would use it as a shared data partition. That is a much safer option than writing to the Windows C: partition directly from Ubuntu. 

sda3 - recovery

sda4 - HP_TOOLS

So - apart from the fact that the available space could be more efficiently disposed between sda5 and sda7, I'd suggest leaving things as they are for now and get some experience with using Ubuntu. Enjoy Ubuntu! Any other questions? :)

**EDIT**: the error message you mention in post #4. Have you seen it again after a reboot?

---

### Post by coffeecat on 2014-05-11
You have a choice whether to save to your home folder or to the data partition. If you open a file manager window, you'll see "ntfs1" in the left pane under "Devices". If you click on that, the partition is automounted and you can copy, save, delete files there more or less as in Windows. If you want to change the partition label, you can do this either with Gparted, or in "Computer" in Windows. 

Ditto all this in Windows, except that you open G:. One thing to beware of. If you hibernate/sleep Windows, then save something to the shared partition in Ubuntu, then start Windows again, Windows will restore the filesystem to the state it was before it was hibernated with the effect of losing everything you saved from Ubuntu. You need to shut down Windows completely if you are intending to save stuff from Ubuntu.

The Gparted error is not something I have seen before. It seems to be grumbling about your sda2 extended partition, although why it should do that escapes me at the moment. I've been installing Ubuntu on logical partitions for years now and I've never seen gparted complain about the extended partition. I'll have a dig around when I have more time and get back to you.
